  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c23da86f-97ae-49e4-9051-1578949c58bd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer14176132396115451623">Magic and murder engulf the realm of Kelewan.  Fierce warlords ignite a bitter blood feud to enslave the empire of Tsuranuanni.  While in the opulent Imperial courts, assassins and spy-master plot cunning and devious intrigues against the rightful heir.  Now Mara, a young, untested Ruling lady, is called upon to lead her people in a heroic struggle for survival.  But first</span>
  <span id="freeText14176132396115451623" style="display:none">Magic and murder engulf the realm of Kelewan.  Fierce warlords ignite a bitter blood feud to enslave the empire of Tsuranuanni.  While in the opulent Imperial courts, assassins and spy-master plot cunning and devious intrigues against the rightful heir.  Now Mara, a young, untested Ruling lady, is called upon to lead her people in a heroic struggle for survival.  But first she must rally an army of rebel warriors, form a pact with the alien cho-ja, and marry the son of a hated enemy.  Only then can Mara face her most dangerous foe of all--in his own impregnable stronghold.  An epic tale of adventure and intrigue.  Daughter of the Empire is fantasy of the highest order by two of the most talented writers in the field today.</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/569668-is-it-okay-to-read-the-empire-trilogy">Is it okay to read The Empire Trilogy before The Riftwar Saga? Are they connected?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_569668'><a id="like_count_community_question_569668" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/569668?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">5 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F589979.Daughter_of_the_Empire">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/569668-is-it-okay-to-read-the-empire-trilogy">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/569668-is-it-okay-to-read-the-empire-trilogy">See all 2 answers</a>
</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/48638435-zchantie"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1447266976i/48638435._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/48638435-zchantie">Zchantie</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I actually did read this series before the Riftwar Saga and was no worse for the wear of it. In many regards I enjoyed the Empire Trilogy more. Most o<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I actually did read this series before the Riftwar Saga and was no worse for the wear of it. In many regards I enjoyed the Empire Trilogy more. Most of what occurs in it is left completely unexplained (and half the time unnoticed) in the Riftwar Saga because there's so much going on. There are cross-over characters but if you blink during the Riftwar Saga you wouldn't even notice the connection. The Empire Trilogy easily stands on its own.<br /><br />Reading the Magician novels of Rift War first would better explain the roles of the Kelewanian magicians that appear in the Empire Trilogy but honestly it's not needed to understand the story.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_553959044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/553959044" />
    <a title="Niki Hawkes - The Obsessive Bookseller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13396599-niki-hawkes---the-obsessive-bookseller"><img alt="Niki Hawkes - The Obsessive Bookseller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517553177p2/13396599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/553959044?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Niki Hawkes - The Obsessive Bookseller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Niki Hawkes - The Obsessive Bookseller" href="/user/show/13396599-niki-hawkes---the-obsessive-bookseller">Niki Hawkes - The Obsessive Bookseller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13396599-niki-hawkes---the-obsessive-bookseller?shelf=my-library-catalogue">my-library-catalogue</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13396599-niki-hawkes---the-obsessive-bookseller?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer553959044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16619610740042695273">Via Book Reviews by Niki Hawkes at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.nikihawkes.com'>www.nikihawkes.com</a><br/><br/>This is a strong contender as my favorite book of all-time. It has all the elements that make a book great: strong characters, excellent world-building, masterful writing, and a page-turning story. If any of you aren’t familiar with Feist’s Riftwar saga, the arc of the series involves one world (Midkemia) being invaded by another world (Kelewan) from across a magical rift. The invading soldiers are known as the Tsurani, and Feist talks a lot a</span>
  <span id="freeText16619610740042695273" style="display:none">Via Book Reviews by Niki Hawkes at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nikihawkes.com">www.nikihawkes.com</a><br /><br />This is a strong contender as my favorite book of all-time. It has all the elements that make a book great: strong characters, excellent world-building, masterful writing, and a page-turning story. If any of you aren’t familiar with Feist’s Riftwar saga, the arc of the series involves one world (Midkemia) being invaded by another world (Kelewan) from across a magical rift. The invading soldiers are known as the Tsurani, and Feist talks a lot about them in his second book, Magician: Master. What the Kelewan Empire trilogy does is take you deep into the Tsurani’s world, following the life of a young woman within it. I LOVED this trilogy. It was such a gripping story that I literally could not put it down!<br /><br />The main character is both dynamic and endearing, and I found her completely fascinating as she develops throughout each book. The world-building was outright fantastic, and arguably the best element of this series. It’s been ten years since I read them, and I can still remember Kelewan in vivid detail. New flora and fauna, new architecture, new politial/religious systems – Feist and Wurts obviously spent a lot of time developing, and really made the world come to life! Because of this trilogy, they are without a doubt my favorite writing duo.<br /><br />Overall, it was an amazing trilogy that I will forever hold on a pedestal. It definitely tops my Best Fantasy Book Bulletin!<br /><br />Recommendations: If you’re new to Feist’s works, I recommend you read Magician: Apprentice and Magician: Master first before switching over to this trilogy, then continue on with “Silverthorn.” Even though these were my all-time favs, the Riftwar saga also makes the top ten list and are definitely worth reading!!<br /><br />by Niki Hawkes<br /><br />Other books you might like:<br /><br />“The Wayfarer Redemption” by Sara Douglass<br />“Medalon” by Jennifer Fallon<br />“The Aware” by Glenda Larke<br />“Thief’s Gamble” by Juliet E. Mckenna<br />“Heart of Myrial” by Maggie Furey</span>

<div id="review_2060099481" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2060099481" />
    <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries"><img alt="Mayim de Vries" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560508357p2/50424456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2060099481?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayim de Vries" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries">Mayim de Vries</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2060099481" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3529153044595884531"><i>“How shall we answer this murder visited upon our house?”</i><br/><br/>I like books about <b>strong, independent women who, despite the adversities of fate, can manage well and do not worry about the growing difficulties and stakes</b>. This is what you can expect from “Daughter of the Empire.”<br/><br/>When Lord Acoma and his son are killed in the war manoeuvred into a suicidal charge by family’s grand enemy, Lord Minwanabi, seventeen-year-old Mara becomes the heiress. The girl is snatched back into the world mere moments fr</span>
  <span id="freeText3529153044595884531" style="display:none"><i>“How shall we answer this murder visited upon our house?”</i><br /><br />I like books about <b>strong, independent women who, despite the adversities of fate, can manage well and do not worry about the growing difficulties and stakes</b>. This is what you can expect from “Daughter of the Empire.”<br /><br />When Lord Acoma and his son are killed in the war manoeuvred into a suicidal charge by family’s grand enemy, Lord Minwanabi, seventeen-year-old Mara becomes the heiress. The girl is snatched back into the world mere moments from becoming a religious initiate. There is no time for any introduction to adulthood. There is no induction period. Her father's and brother's insidious killer is setting snares for her, but also, she lives in a world, where not a deed, but the form matters. It means that she and her family riches are ripe for the taking for everybody intelligent and bold enough to do it within the strict and precise honour system that sits on the Tsuranuanni society like a corset. Fortunately, our heroine is far from a naive novice everybody takes her for; she has a keen mind and a flair for trade and politics. In the Great Game she sets her own rules.<br /><br />OK, it is a cliche <b>“from zero to hero,” but in a female version</b>.<br /><br />The heroine is a very dramatic character in which all elements are mixed up. Mara is fragile, but also courageous, so much, in fact, that she acts taking great risks more than once. She is warm and full of love for those under her care, but she can also coolly calculate her moves even if the pawns in the game are people of flesh and blood. She is pretty but she has sexual issues that make her appear cold and unfeeling. Although she does not have physical strength, she is characterised by an extraordinary analytical mind, cunning and intelligent. She is a shrewd judge of character able to see the opponent’s weaknesses and use them for her purposes. She also does not shy away from difficult, uncomfortable and sometimes dangerous situations or balk when personal sacrifice is necessary. You will be able to watch closely the transformation of a young girl into a strong, determined woman; I really loved this metamorphosis.<br /><br />The main character is the most interesting of those present in the book, but also the secondary protagonists are written in a very expressive way. Each of them is interesting: the nanny and advisor - old Nacoya, courageous and faithful Papewaio; arrogant and somewhat primitive husband Buntokapi <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[whose death was my favourite scene in the book <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, as well as other characters from slaves to lords to spies to sentient insectoids, have been sketched in a truly masterful manner and it is perhaps the creation of the characters that is the greatest advantage of this novel.<br /><br />I am writing “perhaps” because <b>politics and intrigue is the most important and, in fact, the only villain of the whole story</b>. Intrigues, conspirators and murderers are really the main characters of the novel.<br /><br />Granted, <b>the plot seems to be quite naive, but the world and the exquisite culture</b> created by Mr Feist and Ms Wurts renders this novel a unique character. The empire has its own traditions, customs, rituals and beliefs. It is not so much your typical fantasy worlbuilding because the focus is mainly on decisions and their consequences, on subsequent events that allow for further development. Of course, there is magic and six-legged animals, but essentially this world, this society, could exist because the worldbuilding is based on inherent features of human nature… and this alone adds to the charm of this tale. <br /><br />Strong beliefs and rituals based on the Eastern cultures also should be counted among the pros, as they enrich the fibre of the story. Funeral or marriage rites, institutional and societal customs <b>serve as a background for personal decisions and anchor the character developments or plot twists. Everything is consistent and so (I am happy to say so) complex</b> that even the simplest of dialogues or actions, gain surprising depths. <br /><br />I think that if anyone else was writing this novel, he would have been defeated by the concept. However, Mr Feist, as a craftsman in his trade created the framework and let Ms Wurts to breath light and colour into it. While the book is not free from imperfections (mainly because Mara carries the whole weigh of the story and on top of everything she keeps her schemes to herself while her grand-enemy is a bit one-dimensional), but these are not damaging the reading experience; neither too obvious nor spoiling the fun.<br /><br /> A piece of solid, well-written fantasy with a Japanese twist to it: in some way it is a merger between <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17910048.The_Goblin_Emperor__The_Goblin_Emperor___1_" title="The Goblin Emperor (The Goblin Emperor, #1) by Katherine Addison" rel="nofollow noopener">The Goblin Emperor</a> and the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33286244.Chronicle_of_the_Unhewn_Throne__Chronicle_of_the_Unhewn_Throne__1_3_" title="Chronicle of the Unhewn Throne (Chronicle of the Unhewn Throne #1-3) by Brian Staveley" rel="nofollow noopener">Chronicle of the Unhewn Throne</a>. “Daughter of the Empire” is a book about <b>difficult decisions, honour, wild passions, internal strength that is hidden within and perseverance. Too little fighting, too little magic, but enormous amounts of politics, well-written protagonists, and slow-burn but interesting intrigues</b>.<br />_____<br />Also in the series: <br /><br />2. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2697219616" rel="nofollow noopener">Servant of the Empire</a> ★★★☆☆<br />3. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2759737937" rel="nofollow noopener">Mistress of the Empire</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3529153044595884531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer17069913336963953491">I read this years ago and picked it up again because I was into something non-European setting. This is an alt Asain world that is tangental to the Riftworld Saga by Feist, and is a REALLY good read. The protagonist is a young girl who must save her family and lands by growing up very quick. Nothing naughty in here, but lots of great politics a-la Game of Thrones. Highly recommend for a fun and different fantasy!</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_677374806" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/677374806" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/677374806?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people looking for female-centered high/epic fantasy</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to carol.  by: </span>
      Fantasy Aficionados BOM
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=epic-fantasy">epic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=female-lead">female-lead</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer677374806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14394142203475056781">Collaborations can be a challenging way to tell a story, especially when both authors have an established voice. Ultimately, if done well, they are like an interesting ice cream swirl, something of the flavor of both authors creating a pleasant compination. Andre Norton is one of those authors that seem to collaborate well, although I'm not sure if that's partly because she was the idea generator and then had a co-writer do more of the heavy lifting, especially in her later years. One of the bes</span>
  <span id="freeText14394142203475056781" style="display:none">Collaborations can be a challenging way to tell a story, especially when both authors have an established voice. Ultimately, if done well, they are like an interesting ice cream swirl, something of the flavor of both authors creating a pleasant compination. Andre Norton is one of those authors that seem to collaborate well, although I'm not sure if that's partly because she was the idea generator and then had a co-writer do more of the heavy lifting, especially in her later years. One of the best examples of co-writing I remember is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64207.Sorcery___Cecelia_or_The_Enchanted_Chocolate_Pot__Cecelia_and_Kate___1_" title="Sorcery &amp; Cecelia or The Enchanted Chocolate Pot (Cecelia and Kate, #1) by Patricia C. Wrede" rel="nofollow noopener">Sorcery &amp; Cecelia: or The Enchanted Chocolate Pot</a>, largely because it used letters between two young girls as the primary framing device, allowing the voices of both authors to shine. <u>Daughter of the Empire</u> was the result of a collaboration between Janny Wurts and Raymond Feist, and though I haven't read any Feist, I have to say it blends well, and Feist's influence tempered Wurt's writing and made it infinitely more palatable.<br /><br />I've avoided writing about Wurt's books since meeting her in one of my GR groups, as she is a truly wonderful, generous and respectful person. Her contributions to different book discussions are thoughtful and circumspect, and she's willing to share her time if readers are interested. The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/1378935-july-2013-daughter-of-the-empire-discuss-with-janny" rel="nofollow noopener">information she shared</a> about the collaboration was interesting, and gave a great deal of insight into the process. If you've read this book or are a fan of Wurts, I highly recommend checking out her comments.<br /><br />To the book itself: it is a detailed epic fantasy, and would likely appeal to those who are looking for something in the genre that is more female-centered than most. It follows young Mara as she is pulled from a life of religious devotion and into the political games of the local fiefdoms, to both survive and to defend the honor of her house.<br /><br />A note on style: Wurts' writing usually has too many superlatives for my taste, and she ends up resorting to italics in order to make her points in the more emotional sections. It must be Feist's or the editor's influence, because for one of the first times reading her books, I found myself able to concentrate on the story and characterization without being distracted by the writing. In this sense, the book worked for me.<br /><br />Plotting is acceptable. However, Mara develops the habit of keeping her plans to herself, since she doesn't want to argue with her former nursemaid or her man-at-arms. This both aids and detracts the story; aiding because it keeps the reader in suspense about what she will do, but detracting because it means her actions are often not quite comprehensible to the reader. If she explains it over-much to her people. the story risks losing its sense of legitimate dialogue; if it isn't explained, readers are left with a culture and heroine that is just slightly incomprehensible. Thus, after I finished reading, I wasn't sure if I disliked Mara because of a lack of understanding, or because I disliked her because she was a truly dislikeable person. It reminds me, just a little, of the issues I faced in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/276660.Joe_Abercrombie" title="Joe Abercrombie" rel="nofollow noopener">Joe Abercrombie</a>'s First Law series, only he was able to build a sense of complexity that lent itself to compassion. Mara just seems largely incomprehensible, except for the single-minded goal of avenging her family, a fact itself that seems inconsistent with a woman who had left her family for a religious life some time ago.<br /><br />The only spot that the collaboration seemed not to have worked quite as well was the introduction of the cho-ja, the insect-like sentient beings. They played an enormous role in one chapter, and despite "Gaining this new hive would do more to preserve Acoma survival than any dozen clever plots on the High Council," they are only mentioned twice more in the rest of the book. It felt a little intrusive, and when I understood they were of Feist's creation for another aspect of the world, it made more sense. Less for the story, but more sense for the collaboration.<br /><br />One of my largest barriers to enjoyment was Mara's marriage and the subsequent abuse she endured. The cynical part of me wonders if Feist wanted Wurts involved for that perspective alone, and to help legitimize a storyline that was extremely unpalatable. Regardless, my personal issues with that type of plotline are such that I will almost never like a book that involves that kind of abuse, unless done very, very, well. This was not, as it seemed mostly designed to create sympathy for Mara and how her husband was destroying her heritage--sort of. I actually ended up wondering how calculating she really was from the start, which made me like her less. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ It seemed clear that her intent was all along to kill her husband; to be fair, it seemed none of the choices would have been willing co-rulers, but I'm not sure she ever genuinely tried <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />I have other small quibbles that I won't go into, save to note they were there. Ultimately, though I read fantasy, this was not my type of sub-genre, so my thoughts are not predisposed to be generous. Two and a half stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14394142203475056781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5295672310051039243">Ookay, two days later and not quite fully rested up, let's do this.<br/><br/>So. Daughter of the Empire. Yes. Very good book. Highly recommended.<br/>The characters were excellent, really. The setting was vaguely oriental, (though as Wastrel tells me, Wurts drew a lot from Korea) so a large part of this book is political intrigue and matters of honor. If that's your team, go for it.   <span id="freeText5295672310051039243" style="display:none">Ookay, two days later and not quite fully rested up, let's do this.<br /><br />So. Daughter of the Empire. Yes. Very good book. Highly recommended.<br />The characters were excellent, really. The setting was vaguely oriental, (though as Wastrel tells me, Wurts drew a lot from Korea) so a large part of this book is political intrigue and matters of honor. If that's your team, go for it. If the whole honor thing isn't for you, maybe not as much. But the political intrigue stuff is actually really excellently written.<br /><br />Moving on. Mara. Oh, Mara. Mara was magnificent. Mara was fantastic. I could sing praises for Mara for weeks. Or months.<br />Mara is that strong female character you've always wished you'd find somewhere in fantasy. She's beautifully real and it's <i>very</i> easy to empathize with her.<br /><br />The supporting characters really made the book for me. Wurts and Feist didn't let anything slip by them; each of their supporting characters were wonderfully realized and fully fleshed-out.<br /><br />Ugh. Highly recommended. Highly.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570545216?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570545216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer482136161465110680">Having re-read the first four books in the Riftwar series, I'm FINALLY going to continue on with the full series in the order recommended. <br/><br/>I'll admit I was a bit skeptical about the collaboration because I was already a fan of Feist and simply wanted all the cool craziness and awesome ideas breaching time and space and two universes in his epic fantasy setup. :)<br/><br/>Moving on... this isn't that. It IS set on the world where Pug learned his mastery of magic, but beyond that and the focus on Korean-ish</span>
  <span id="freeText482136161465110680" style="display:none">Having re-read the first four books in the Riftwar series, I'm FINALLY going to continue on with the full series in the order recommended. <br /><br />I'll admit I was a bit skeptical about the collaboration because I was already a fan of Feist and simply wanted all the cool craziness and awesome ideas breaching time and space and two universes in his epic fantasy setup. :)<br /><br />Moving on... this isn't that. It IS set on the world where Pug learned his mastery of magic, but beyond that and the focus on Korean-ish politics and The Game between houses, there's not any magic to speak of.<br /><br />Mara is our focus, inheriting and learning to defend her house and its lands after her father dies. From start to finish, it's all about playing The Game. Survival by way of alliances, treachery, honor, and politics.<br /><br />For what it is, namely a fantasy showcasing the rise of a single woman in an alien empire, letting us grow with her in the process, how she gains alliances, gains and loses a husband, and survives debts, and the matter of a blood feud. Pretty cool, all told, but I should mention one thing...<br /><br />Even tho this came out many years ago, there have been MANY authors to follow in this particular footstep, and I'm pretty sure that this duo was not the first. If I had to rate some scenes between, say, Brandon Sanderson's Stormlight books and this, I would have to point to Sanderson... BUT, some awesome scenes first showed up in Daughter of the Empire. Just saying.<br /><br />I may not love this nearly as much as I enjoyed the first four books, but I definitely enjoyed myself reading this. Mara is a strong female character dominating a tale back when it was still a rarity. :)<br /><br />I'll be devouring two more of these in the trilogy in a few days. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="482136161465110680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2288906847?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia" href="/user/show/2445786-olivia">Olivia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/17284950-daughter-of-the-empire">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2445786-olivia?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2288906847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16201988679285521496">What a wonderful book. Daughter of the Empire is set in a world inspired by Asia. There's not a lot of magic, instead it's all about intrigue and politics. In addition, expect a kick-ass female character. <br/><br/>Mara is simply one of the best female fantasy characters I've ever encountered. She is cunning and intelligent. This entire book is one big chess game filled with politics and betrayal, and Mara does not use strength and violence to win this game but her wit. It's a joy to watch her decimate he</span>
  <span id="freeText16201988679285521496" style="display:none">What a wonderful book. Daughter of the Empire is set in a world inspired by Asia. There's not a lot of magic, instead it's all about intrigue and politics. In addition, expect a kick-ass female character. <br /><br />Mara is simply one of the best female fantasy characters I've ever encountered. She is cunning and intelligent. This entire book is one big chess game filled with politics and betrayal, and Mara does not use strength and violence to win this game but her wit. It's a joy to watch her decimate her opponents using only her brain. More of this, please.<br /><br />The entire cast of characters is well developed. The villains are intriguing and relatable. On one page you want to punch them, on the next they break your heart! There was one scene especially which was a punch in the gut, and only a few pages prior I thought I'd be throwing a party.<br /><br />I recommend this to anyone who loves fantasy with a lot of politics and intrigue. If several houses attempting to outwit each other in a bid to rule appeals to you, then this is for you. <b>If you're at all interested in Feist's work and the rest of the Riftwar Cycle, start with the Riftwar Saga Trilogy before you dive into the Empire Trilogy.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="16201988679285521496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_175941536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175941536" />
    <a title="Pauline Ross" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3321575-pauline-ross"><img alt="Pauline Ross" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442980323p2/3321575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175941536?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pauline Ross" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pauline Ross" href="/user/show/3321575-pauline-ross">Pauline Ross</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586074817" href="/book/show/43907.Daughter_of_the_Empire">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3321575-pauline-ross?shelf=2-star">2-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3321575-pauline-ross?shelf=genre-fantasy">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3321575-pauline-ross?shelf=bookgroup1112">bookgroup1112</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3321575-pauline-ross?shelf=actual-book">actual-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3321575-pauline-ross?shelf=year-2012">year-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175941536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15984183111837165565">This book ought to have been right up my street; a non-medieval world, with limited magic, a slow pace driven by politics rather than endless battles, and a strong-minded female lead - what's not to like? In my case, the answer is: almost everything.<br/><br/>The opening felt surprisingly clunky and uncertain. Many fantasy works start with a dramatic event to draw the reader in, and leave the details of the background to wait for a quieter moment, but this tries to do both at once, with unconvincing resul</span>
  <span id="freeText15984183111837165565" style="display:none">This book ought to have been right up my street; a non-medieval world, with limited magic, a slow pace driven by politics rather than endless battles, and a strong-minded female lead - what's not to like? In my case, the answer is: almost everything.<br /><br />The opening felt surprisingly clunky and uncertain. Many fantasy works start with a dramatic event to draw the reader in, and leave the details of the background to wait for a quieter moment, but this tries to do both at once, with unconvincing results. To some extent it's necessary to explain the unusually high levels of required etiquette, which is required to account for the protagonist's unnatural calmness in a crisis, but the authors simultaneously tried to convey her deep emotions, and this made for a very uneven few chapters. And somehow the situation felt rather forced to me. Are there really no other members of the family? This seems oddly shortsighted in a hereditary system (lots of babies, people; how hard is that to understand?).<br /><br />The whole story rests on the shoulders of Mara, the seventeen year old protagonist. Given that the story opens with her about to take lifelong vows at a religious order, she seems initially unsuited for the leadership role thrust upon her, and perhaps that would have been a more interesting line to follow. However, it soon becomes clear that she is the conventional spirited and ambitious protagonist, quite prepared to be ruthless to achieve her political aims, and also prepared to take risks and break with tradition where she deems it necessary. Unfortunately, this very ruthlessness makes her something of an unsympathetic character. She has no qualms, it seems, about killing off slaves, rebellious soldiers or anyone else who gets in her way, or simply to send a message to her rivals. And considering that the concept of honour is deeply embedded in this society, she has (in fact, they all have) a very curious sense of honour which allows her to do (apparently) whatever the hell she wants.<br /><br />The other main characters are all very black and white. Her family servants are the ultimate in loyal retainers, willing to die for her, but also intelligent, resourceful, competent... you get the picture. Her husband, carefully selected solely to create a protective alliance and give Mara an heir, is portrayed as an uncaring and violent brute. Some attempt is made to paint a more subtle picture of Buntokapi later on, but it's really too late by this time. Her enemies are no more than ciphers: the rival leader, driven by long-standing feuds whose origins are lost in time; the stupid men driven by lust; the vengeful woman. Only the Warlord has seems like a genuinely complex character. The rest are no more than lightly sketched background the better to illuminate Mara's perfection .<br /><br />And here we come to the real problem with this book. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Everything Mara does, no matter how dangerous or risky, turns out implausibly well. This really isn't much of a spoiler, actually, since it's obvious right from the start that she will ultimately triumph, but I expected her to have some setbacks along the way. But no, everything works out exactly as she planned it and, honestly, this simply isn't interesting. She needs more warriors, so she goes out to recruit outlaws. She needs the cho-ja, she gets them. She wants her husband dead, so he dies. This was the final straw, for me. There were only three possible outcomes to Mara's nasty little plot against Buntokapi: global war, family war or the brutish, stupid husband who beats up his own wife suddenly turns all honourable and commits ritual suicide. I mean, really? All at once, he's being noble? And this man who can't control his own temper comes back to the wife who set him up and says magnanimously, 'Well, that was very clever, wife, you beat me fair and square there.' If it were me, I'd have gutted her first, and honour be hanged. What an evil witch. Ugh.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />So at this point I'm seriously disappointed. But then there's a seemingly trivial interaction between two supposedly minor characters, at the end of which there's a reveal which changes everything. All of a sudden the political shenanigans become much more complicated and very interesting indeed. For a moment the book gleams with possibilities. Sadly the rest of the book isn't like this. And in fact, immediately after this we're back to Mara being terribly clever and underhanded and devious. I would like her a bit better if she were not so downright nasty to people. Yes, I know she has reasons to be cautious of anyone approaching her, but surely the honour system would deny the sort of ritual humiliation she indulges in.<br /><br />I was really looking forward to reading this. It presses all the right buttons for me. I like a world that's not a mud-bespattered caricature of the middle ages. I like the system of honour and the rigid protocol. I like a book which builds slowly without leaping from one set-piece battle to the next. I like a story built around characters. And yet none of it worked for me. The protocol and honour seemed to be no more than a clever plot device. The slowness of the book made it draggy at times - there were whole chapters devoted to seemingly trivial events which I lost interest in long before their purpose was revealed. And I hated Mara - really, really hated her. Are we actually supposed to root for a heroine who devises such unpleasant - no, downright evil schemes? There is nothing at all likeable, to my mind, about such a cruelly manipulative person. I can see the attraction in a woman facing up to adversity with spirit and determination and yes, even ruthlessness, so other readers may enjoy it much better, but it's not for me. So two stars for effort, I suppose, grudgingly.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15984183111837165565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2257525917397442688" style="display:none">I re-read this for the 3d or 5th time, but it's been at least a decade, so it was very fresh to me. Too fresh &amp; interesting. <div id="review_2475788201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2475788201" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36502262-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501438390p2/36502262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2475788201?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/36502262-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36502262-eric?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36502262-eric?shelf=raymond-e-feist">raymond-e-feist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2475788201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9281922409845982859">4 Stars<br/><br/>Filled with scheming and intrigue, Daughter of the Empire starts this Riftwar spinoff series strong. The story follows Mara, a young woman forced unexpectedly into leading her House through a dangerous game of politics as enemies circle, waiting to destroy her and her people. <br/><br/>There’s a lot of things to like in this book. First, the setting is fantastic. Set in the Tsurani culture, it’s very different to your average fantasy world. The culture has a lot of Asian elements and revolves aroun</span>
  <span id="freeText9281922409845982859" style="display:none">4 Stars<br /><br />Filled with scheming and intrigue, Daughter of the Empire starts this Riftwar spinoff series strong. The story follows Mara, a young woman forced unexpectedly into leading her House through a dangerous game of politics as enemies circle, waiting to destroy her and her people. <br /><br />There’s a lot of things to like in this book. First, the setting is fantastic. Set in the Tsurani culture, it’s very different to your average fantasy world. The culture has a lot of Asian elements and revolves around your House and family honor. There are tons of little flourishes and traditions that make the culture feel real, and the characters are all heavily influenced by the code of honor that holds their society together. The characters themselves are interesting, from Mara, the young ruling Lady, to her veteran commander to her old advisor to the various other scheming lords and rulers. <br /><br />As the cover blurb alludes to, this book focuses heavily on politics, which in the Tsurani world is just as deadly as open combat. While at times I would have liked a little variety from the political intrigue, it was cleverly written and kept the tension high.<br /><br />The book is written in such a way that if you haven’t read the Riftwar books, everything can still make sense. I would advise to read those books first, though, as it’ll give some additional depth and you’ll pick up on a few more things. Also, if I didn’t already know the culture and history of the Tsurani world, the beginning might have been a bit of a struggle until I got acclimatized. Besides, if you haven’t read Feist you’re missing out on some great classic fantasy. <br /><br />Growing up, Feist’s Riftwar books where one of my main introductions to fantasy, and I read and reread them an embarrassing amount of times. For some reason I never picked up this series though. I think I was put off by the political intrigue focus, when I was younger that type of story didn’t interest me much. I’m glad I decided to come back and give these books a chance. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9281922409845982859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_202279211" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202279211" />
    <a title="Kay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5611008-kay"><img alt="Kay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314169555p2/5611008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202279211?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kay" href="/user/show/5611008-kay">Kay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=formidable-women">formidable-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5611008-kay?shelf=power-games">power-games</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202279211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer203127549965024546">It's been a while since I've read a story with such complex political intrigue, and I enjoyed every word of it! <br/><br/><b>
  <u>Plot Summary</u>
</b><br/><br/>Set in a Japanese-style setting and culture, Mara of the Acoma is but a few minutes away from being initiated into the service of the goddess Lashima. She is, however, rudely jerked from her chosen path when news of her father's and brother's death reaches her household. In Mara's world, power is determined by the <b>Game of the Council</b>, the neverending power struggle mask</span>
  <span id="freeText203127549965024546" style="display:none">It's been a while since I've read a story with such complex political intrigue, and I enjoyed every word of it! <br><br><b>
  <u>Plot Summary</u>
</b><br><br>Set in a Japanese-style setting and culture, Mara of the Acoma is but a few minutes away from being initiated into the service of the goddess Lashima. She is, however, rudely jerked from her chosen path when news of her father's and brother's death reaches her household. In Mara's world, power is determined by the <b>Game of the Council</b>, the neverending power struggle masked by a facade of courtesy--kind of like, let me respectfully bow and serve you chilled fruit while I run you through with this sword. <br><br><span>[image error]</span><br><br><i>No need to be rude while pillaging.</i><br><br>Mara must use all her wiles and courage to defeat the powerful Minwanabi house, whose members ordered the death of her family, and to rebuild her house to become one of the most powerful in the empire.<br><br> <br><b>
  <u>Reactions</u>
</b><br><br>This book has everything to satisfy any reader looking for political plotting thicker than molasses and intense behind-the-curtains wheeling and dealing. Plus, add in a few assassinations and underhanded trickery, and you've got a nice manual for your inner budding Machiavelli.<br><br>Though this book is an extension of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8588.Raymond_E__Feist" title="Raymond E. Feist" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond E. Feist</a>'s Riftwar Series, I never felt lost or felt like I lacked vital information about the world. Kelewan is a refreshing change of scenery from 90% of fantasy's Western-style worlds. And despite my meme above, I imagined settings like this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380357670i/708894._SX540_.jpg" width='410"' height="307" class="gr-hostedUserImg">.<br><br>Governed by strict honor and warrior codes, Kelewans are fierce in their observance of duty and conduct to the point of choosing death over dishonor. <br><br>Much of this book revolves around Mara's development from an experienced young girl to a powerful political figure and leader. And believe you me, her path is not one filled with flowers and rainbows. She endures violence, abuse, frustrations, and then some, but still has the wits to emerge victorious. Mara particularly shines in her flexibility with bending Kelewan's codes of conduct to gain advantage over her enemies.<br><br>The characters were also superbly developed. Mara's complexity and vitality goes without saying. Her inner strength and intelligence reminded me a lot of Phedre from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/153008.Kushiel_s_Dart__Ph_dre_s_Trilogy__1_" title="Kushiel's Dart (Phèdre's Trilogy #1) by Jacqueline Carey" rel="nofollow noopener">Kushiel's Dart</a> and Catelyn from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13496.A_Game_of_Thrones__A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire___1_" title="A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, #1) by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">Game of Thrones</a>. But I also need to give a shout out to the violent, cunning, bullying <b>Bunto</b>. He's ignorant and seemingly beyond redemption, but he became one of the most human "villains" I've ever read about just because his ignorance is so common yet real. <br><br>What I found offsetting was that the numerous codes seemed spontaneously introduced to manipulate the plot. The concept of conducting oneself courtiously and honorably is pretty much imprinted on the mind by page 50, but even at page 400, we still get schooled on <i>how to pleasantly receive enemy house guests</i> or <i>how deep to bow to offer just the right amount of offense</i>. The constant reminder of these rules became invasive to an otherwise naturally developing story. <br><br>Overall, <b>4.5 STARS</b> and HIGHLY RECOMMENDED. If you're a fan of political intrigue and strong female characters, this is your book. <br><br>Now, onto <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13815.Servant_of_the_Empire__The_Empire_Trilogy___2_" title="Servant of the Empire (The Empire Trilogy, #2) by Raymond E. Feist" rel="nofollow noopener">Servant of the Empire</a>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="203127549965024546" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2977451305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2977451305" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2977451305?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35688992-daughter-of-the-empire">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2977451305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer283380934396316892"><i>Daughter of the Empire</i> is the first book in <i>The Empire Trilogy</i> by Raymond E. Feist and Janny Wurts. It takes place at the same time as <i>Magician</i> from the <i>Riftwar Saga</i>, but it’s set on a different world and focuses on different characters. There are a few references to the first subseries, but it would be easy to jump in here without any familiarity with the other books.<br/><br/>The story focuses primarily on Mara, a seventeen-year-old girl whose father and brother, not to mention a large portion of their </span>
  <span id="freeText283380934396316892" style="display:none"><i>Daughter of the Empire</i> is the first book in <i>The Empire Trilogy</i> by Raymond E. Feist and Janny Wurts. It takes place at the same time as <i>Magician</i> from the <i>Riftwar Saga</i>, but it’s set on a different world and focuses on different characters. There are a few references to the first subseries, but it would be easy to jump in here without any familiarity with the other books.<br /><br />The story focuses primarily on Mara, a seventeen-year-old girl whose father and brother, not to mention a large portion of their military, are killed when an enemy house betrays them. Mara learns of this at the very beginning of the book, just as she’s in the middle of a ceremony to be initiated into a religious order. Mara is the only one left alive to lead her house, so her future is about to change drastically, if she can survive leading a house with depleted military forces and enemies determined to see her house fall.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this. The story held my attention from beginning to end, and I felt like it had more depth and nuance as compared to the first subseries. There are a lot more shades of gray, both in the plot and in the characters. Mara is an easy character to sympathize with and root for, and I couldn’t help but cheer her every success, but at the same time I often felt uncomfortable about some things she did to achieve those successes. She isn’t a character I can love with blind devotion like I do some of the characters from the <i>Riftwar Saga</i>, but she feels real and she’s a lot of fun to read about. I loved her cleverness and her boldness, and her ability to adapt to a role that her upbringing had not prepared her for.<br /><br />I also very much liked some of the other characters. The story is full of politics and scheming and some military action. I really didn’t think there were any slow parts, although it never <i>quite</i> became a compulsive read that I couldn’t put down. I’m rating this at 4.5 stars and rounding up to 5 on Goodreads. Despite being the first book in a trilogy, this book tells a complete story and would be satisfying on its own. I, however, am moving straight on to the next book. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="283380934396316892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1738403254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738403254" />
    <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade"><img alt="Stevie Kincade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476505168p2/56187822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1738403254?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stevie Kincade" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade">Stevie Kincade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1738403254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14535804358148434795">I am a huge fan of Raymond Feist's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43916.Magician?from_search=true' rel='nofollow noopener'>Magician</a> because it is one of those rare stories that doesn't have a beginning, middle and end. It doesn't follow the typical conflict/resolution setup and like the best stories it continues way past resolving of "the big conflict" and doesn't end neatly or suddenly like so many stories do. I was disappointed that after writing that masterpiece Feist apparently took a course on "how to write a book" and all of his stories followed the template of "The hero must</span>
  <span id="freeText14535804358148434795" style="display:none">I am a huge fan of Raymond Feist's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43916.Magician?from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Magician</a> because it is one of those rare stories that doesn't have a beginning, middle and end. It doesn't follow the typical conflict/resolution setup and like the best stories it continues way past resolving of "the big conflict" and doesn't end neatly or suddenly like so many stories do. I was disappointed that after writing that masterpiece Feist apparently took a course on "how to write a book" and all of his stories followed the template of "The hero must complete a quest in 3 parts and will face roadblocks A, B and C before overcoming them". After finishing the Riftwar saga I was let down that the series never lived up the wide open unconventional ideas Feist showed with his debut.<br /><br />This was my first time reading anything by Janny Wurts and this book was recommended to me due to my love of "court intrigue"/manners books with little or no magic. <br /><br />This book ties into the "Riftwar saga"...barely - with mention of the Warlord, metal shortages and the Barbarians beyond the rift but there wasn't much of a payoff. The world felt really bland and nondescript. When the insect aliens were introduced I got briefly excited that some world building might take place but they were never referred to again. I can only assume they will be important in a later book.<br /><br />On the positive side I never found the story boring or slow paced. There was always a goal or a problem for Mara to solve and I was happy to find out what that was until the next story nugget came along. In other words that "A then B then C" plot structure I hated in the Riftwar sequels was less apparent. <br /><br />Narrator Tania Rodrigues was OK. Maybe a little below average in the acting department but she didn't distract from the story all that much.<br /><br />This was a solid, workmanlike court intrigue. It was pretty simple to follow with obvious bad guys and no need to remember 17 house names and factions. Mara used a good combination of guile and chastely womanish charms to get her way. I thought the way she willingly waded into the death star at the end was a bit much but yup that's what heroes do I guess. I'm not sure if I will continue the series or not, it was fine but I think I'd prefer to continue with the Jaqueline Carey court intrigues.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14535804358148434795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2675189911" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2675189911" />
    <a title="Narilka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60022503-narilka"><img alt="Narilka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475501572p2/60022503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2675189911?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Narilka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Narilka" href="/user/show/60022503-narilka">Narilka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60022503-narilka?shelf=fbr">fbr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60022503-narilka?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2675189911" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9337735603742315579">Rating: 3.5 stars<br/><br/>As the only daughter of Lord of the Acoma, Mara was expected to marry and bear children to continue the Acoma line and bring honor to her house. Instead, Mara has chosen a life of chastity and service of Lashmina, the Lady, Goddess of Inner Light. In the temple of Lashima seventeen-year-old Mara is about to take her oaths and join the Order of Lashima. Before the last gongs can sound there is a commotion in the temple as a warrior breaks through with news. Mara's father and brot</span>
  <span id="freeText9337735603742315579" style="display:none">Rating: 3.5 stars<br /><br />As the only daughter of Lord of the Acoma, Mara was expected to marry and bear children to continue the Acoma line and bring honor to her house. Instead, Mara has chosen a life of chastity and service of Lashmina, the Lady, Goddess of Inner Light. In the temple of Lashima seventeen-year-old Mara is about to take her oaths and join the Order of Lashima. Before the last gongs can sound there is a commotion in the temple as a warrior breaks through with news. Mara's father and brother are dead to treachery of a rival house. Mara must return home and take up her inheritance as Ruling Lady of the Acoma lest her house fall into ruin. Untried and untested Mara now must lead her people in her society's complex game of honor among the great houses if the Acoma are to have a future. Let the Game of the Council begin!<br /><br /><i>Daughter of the Empire</i> is the first in the <i>Empire Trilogy</i> by Raymond E. Feist and Janny Wurts. Overlapping the timeline of the Riftwar, we get a look into the life and world on the other side of the Rift in the empire of Tsuranuanni. It has been many years since I read the Riftwar quartet so I was a little concerned going in that I would be missing out on something as I don't remember many details at this point. Turns out that this book only has a couple mentions of the war and the context for what you need to know are given in the book. I would say you can probably read these first though I imagine you'll have a deeper experience if you read the series in publishing order and closer together than I did.<br /><br />The Empire of Tsuranuanni has a lot of Asian influences with touches of Mayan/Incan cultures. The society is based on Great Houses and heavily caste based with a highly complicated and strict system of honor. The people of the world hold their code of honor so high they will often choose death over dishonor. It also makes navigating society an interesting game, the Game of the Council as it's called, as the slightest change in expression or wording can take people from being the best of friends to the most hated of enemies. While the rules are not well explained I felt like I understood it well enough to follow along with Mara's decision making in her society's context.<br /><br />The story is told from Mara's point of view. She is the type of heroine I love in fantasy. She meets her challenges head on and proves she's up to the task. Highly intelligent, she constantly proves that people underestimate her abilities at political intrigue. She tackles her problems logically and it's with her rigid political structure in mind that she's able to manipulate events to turn in her favor. She goes on quite a character arc that by the end of the book most of Tusarni society knows that Mara of the Acoma is a force to be reckoned with.<br /><br />Overall this was an intriguing read. There were some great moments though it is a slow burn in general as the story takes a lot of setup for each situation to come to fruition. It made for some uneven pacing as I blew through some parts and the book was easy to set aside others. There were also a couple other minor annoyances that I won't get into save to note they are there. I enjoyed the book enough I will definitely be continuing on to the second.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9337735603742315579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Mara starts off the book as this innocent, naive, anxious teenage girl, then a few days later she's turned into this conniving, cunning, political player who is besting everyone - including men who have been playing at dangerous politics for their entire lives.<br /><br />2. Mara never tells anyone her plans - even when it would be helpful to her protectors and friends<br /><br />3. Too many coincidental things happen - every time Mara has a problem things go the absolute perfect way for her <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ for example:<br />- she needs men and "poof" all the grey soldiers become hers - including one of the best spy masters in the country<br />- Bunto just happens to come home in a rage and says the exact right thing needed for Mara to have ammunition to end him ... oh, and the Warlord and his father are both on his way and no one tells him - the timing and circumstances just don't hold up for me<br />- the same goes for the neighbor who owed her a small amount of money. She risks her life over a small debt, the entire implausible plan goes her way<br />- She goes to the Warlord's birthday celebration, into the very heart of the enemy, she manages to get the right intelligence, things happen in a really convenient way and her enemy dies<br /><br />4.         rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3080893-sandra?shelf=audio">audio</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111150124" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16024027791587963201">There's a discussion going on right now at Goodreads about whether magic is necessary to a fantasy novel, and I have to say that this one certainly proves that it's not at all necessary. The realm of Kelewan is on the other side of the 'rift' from the realm of Midkemia that we were introduced to in the four Riftwar novels leading up to this one. It's a fully formed land with strange (to us) flora and fauna and other races (the choja). While it has an oriental flavor, it is also original and fasc</span>
  <span id="freeText16024027791587963201" style="display:none">There's a discussion going on right now at Goodreads about whether magic is necessary to a fantasy novel, and I have to say that this one certainly proves that it's not at all necessary. The realm of Kelewan is on the other side of the 'rift' from the realm of Midkemia that we were introduced to in the four Riftwar novels leading up to this one. It's a fully formed land with strange (to us) flora and fauna and other races (the choja). While it has an oriental flavor, it is also original and fascinating in many ways.<br /><br />This novel can also address the issue of whether a female can be the hero of a fantasy series. Mara, Lady of Acoma, is a strong heroine, indeed.<br /><br />The story is about Mara's attempts to maintain honor and power for her family after her father and brother are killed by treachery in a battle between the great families that feud and struggle for power in this realm. The shifts and currents and political and psychological maneuvering that take place in the great Game of the Council that is fascinating, exhilarating, and always deadly - for someone.<br /><br />Magic appears in this book only in the end, in a ritual devised by the Great Ones or magicians who exist in Kelewan only to serve the empire.<br /><br />Now I can't wait to read the next one!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1164519068111616263">I didn't particularly enjoy this one. Perhaps my expectations were a bit high after loving The Riftwar Saga as much as I did.<br/><br/>For me it felt a lot like I was watching someone play a game that I didn't know the rules of. I think </span>
  <span id="freeText1164519068111616263" style="display:none">I didn't particularly enjoy this one. Perhaps my expectations were a bit high after loving The Riftwar Saga as much as I did.<br /><br />For me it felt a lot like I was watching someone play a game that I didn't know the rules of. I knew that Mara was plotting something and her seemingly unwise behaviour was motivated by some secret scheme she had planned, but since I didn't know all the rules within the culture of the Tsurani I couldn't possibly foresee how her plans might work out until they did. I think her cons would have seemed more impressive if I'd understood better what constraints she acted within, but without prior knowledge it all fell flat for me. <br /><br />The story was a bit slow and although there were some good moments and interesting ruses, overall it didn't hold my interest. I probably won't read the rest of this trilogy, but will still read other trilogies in the Riftwar Cycle. </span>
